> (a) Absolute tracking error. (b) Integrated squared error.
> Figure 11

Sensitivity of surrogate AOT performance to the feedback-resolution parameter ℎ in the solution-map learning route. in data quality. Figure 12 shows that larger training sets generally lead to better tracking. This is consistent with the tracking analysis in Theorem 3.8: improving the approximation of the solution map reduces the surrogate residuals and hence lowers the tracking error floor. In the noisy setting, the same trend remains visible, although it is partially masked by the observation-noise floor. 

6.4 Discussion of numerical findings 

Our numerical experiments support the main qualitative predictions of the tracking analysis. The exact-model nudged dynamics exhibit the expected dependence on the feedback resolution and nudging strength. In both learning routes, surrogate AOT substantially improves over the corresponding free learned dynamics, showing that the AOT algorithm remains effective when the exact drift is replaced by a learned surrogate. The sensitivity studies are also consistent with the theory: finer feedback resolution and larger training sets improve long-time tracking. Compared with the dictionary-learning route, the solution-map learning route displays a more visible residual error floor, consistent with its use of a generic ReLU approximation rather than a nearly realizable, problem-specific dictionary.

> Figure 12

Sensitivity of surrogate AOT performance to the training sample size in the solution-map learning route. 

# 7 Conclusions 

This paper developed a unified finite-dimensional framework for continuous data assimilation with learned surrogate dynamics. We analyzed an exact-model AOT baseline under structural assumptions on the dynamics and the feedback operator, rather than using model-specific arguments. Within this framework, we established global well-posedness, post-absorption bounds for the true and nudged systems, and exponential tracking guarantees in both the noise-free and noisy-observation settings. We then incorporated learned surrogate dynamics into the AOT framework and quantified how the resulting model error affects synchronization. This gives explicit tracking bounds in which the long-time error can be controlled by local surrogate residuals on the post-absorption region and, in the stochastic setting, by the observation noise. For constructing AOT-valid surrogates, we provided two learning routes, direct vector-field learn-ing and short-time solution-map learning, and derived checkable criteria linking the corresponding learning errors to the residual quantities that enter the tracking estimates, without necessarily requiring explicit prior structural knowledge of the force field. We further provided learning-theoretic guarantees showing that such surrogates can be learned from finite samples while preserving the long-time synchronization mechanism, thereby tying the statistical analysis directly to the dynamical criterion required by the AOT tracking theory. The main results are formulated in a finite-dimensional setting, where the standing assumptions in Section 2.1 directly imply global well-posedness and the equivalence of the 𝐻 - and 𝑉 -norms turns 

𝐻 -level post-absorption bounds into the 𝑉 -level bounds needed for the tracking estimates. These properties are no longer automatic in an infinite-dimensional Hilbert space setting. Nevertheless, the underlying well-posedness and tracking arguments are based on energy estimates and are not intrinsically finite-dimensional. Thus, in an infinite-dimensional formulation where the true system is known to admit a global strong solution at the required regularity level, the well-posedness of the corresponding nudged and surrogate-nudged systems can be obtained under similar structural assumptions by standard Galerkin approximation and energy-estimate arguments. If, in addition, the required post-absorption 𝑉 -bounds are available, either by assumption or by separate 𝑉 -level energy estimates, then the deterministic tracking and surrogate-tracking results of Sections 2 and 3 carry 

39 over in the same form, with the same type of conditions on 𝜇 and ℎ, up to changes in the constants. This perspective is consistent with the original AOT framework [ 6], fluid and geophysical models [10 , 8, 29 ], and abstract semilinear parabolic formulations [ 20 ]. The same interpretation applies to the noisy-observation results when the stochastic forcing is well defined and the quadratic-variation term in the energy estimate is finite. By contrast, the sample-complexity results in Section 5 remain finite-dimensional. Extending them to infinite-dimensional input spaces and surrogate classes requires distinct approximation and statistical-complexity theory, which we leave for future work.

45 Verification for finite-dimensional Galerkin-type models of viscous incompressible flows . We verify that a standard class of finite-dimensional Galerkin-type models for viscous incompressible flows fits into the abstract framework of Section 2. Consider reduced models of the form 

˙𝑢 + 𝜈 Λ𝑢 + B(𝑢, 𝑢 ) = 𝑔, 𝑢 (𝑡 ) ∈ R𝑑 , (A.1) where Λ is symmetric positive definite and B : R𝑑 × R𝑑 → R𝑑 is bilinear. The verification below uses the standard incompressible energy-cancellation identity 

⟨B(𝑧, 𝑧 ), 𝑧 ⟩𝐻 = 0 , ∀ 𝑧 ∈ R𝑑 . (A.2) This structure arises, for example, from Fourier–Galerkin truncations of the two-dimensional Navier–Stokes equations on a periodic domain, and more generally from Galerkin reductions based on an 𝐿 2-orthonormal divergence-free reduced velocity space with boundary conditions preserving the incompressible energy identity, such as periodic, no-penetration, or no-slip conditions. The 

𝐿 2-orthonormality allows the 𝐿 2-energy of the reduced velocity field to be written as the Euclidean energy of its coefficient vector in R𝑑 , while the divergence-free and boundary assumptions remove the pressure contribution and the boundary term in the convective energy balance. To indicate the origin of the bilinear term, let 𝑋 𝑑 = span {𝜙 1, . . . , 𝜙 𝑑 } be an 𝐿 2-orthonormal divergence-free reduced velocity space satisfying the above boundary assumptions, and write

> 𝑑

> 𝑗 =1

The second term vanishes because 𝑢 𝑧 is divergence-free, and the boundary term vanishes under the stated periodic, no-penetration, or no-slip boundary conditions. Hence (A.2) holds. We now verify Assumptions 2.1, 2.2, and 2.3. Set 

𝐻 = 𝑉 = R𝑑 , 𝐴 = Λ , 𝑓 = 𝑔, 𝑁 (𝑢 ) = B(𝑢, 𝑢 ).

Since Λ is symmetric positive definite, Assumption 2.1 holds immediately. For Assumption 2.2, using (A.2), we obtain, for any 𝑧 ∈ R𝑑 ,

𝜈 𝑧 ⊤𝐴𝑧 + ⟨𝑁 (𝑧 ), 𝑧 ⟩𝐻 = 𝜈 𝑧 ⊤Λ𝑧 ≥ 𝜈 𝜆 min (Λ) ‖𝑧 ‖2 

> 𝐻

.

Thus Assumption 2.2 holds with 𝛼 = 𝜈𝜆 min (Λ) , 𝛽 = 0 .

We now verify Assumption 2.3. First, 

⟨𝑁 (𝑧 ), 𝑧 ⟩𝐻 = ⟨B(𝑧, 𝑧 ), 𝑧 ⟩𝐻 = 0 , ∀ 𝑧 ∈ R𝑑 .

46 Thus Assumption 2.3 (i) holds with 𝐶 E = 0 .It remains to verify the local Lipschitz property and Assumption 2.3 (ii) . Since B is bilinear on the finite-dimensional space R𝑑 , there is a constant 𝐶 B > 0 such that 

‖B(𝑧, 𝑧 ′)‖2 ≤ 𝐶 B‖𝑧 ‖2‖𝑧 ′‖2, ∀ 𝑧, 𝑧 ′ ∈ R𝑑 .

Therefore, 

𝑁 (𝑧 ′) − 𝑁 (𝑧 ) = B(𝑧 ′ − 𝑧, 𝑧 ′) + B(𝑧, 𝑧 ′ − 𝑧 ),

and hence 

‖𝑁 (𝑧 ′) − 𝑁 (𝑧 )‖2 ≤ 𝐶 B(︀ ‖𝑧 ‖2 + ‖𝑧 ′‖2)︀ ‖𝑧 ′ − 𝑧 ‖2.

On the ball ‖𝑧 ‖2, ‖𝑧 ′‖2 ≤ 𝑅 , this gives 

‖𝑁 (𝑧 ′) − 𝑁 (𝑧 )‖2 ≤ 2𝐶 B𝑅 ‖𝑧 ′ − 𝑧 ‖2.

In particular, 𝑁 is locally Lipschitz on R𝑑 . Moreover, 

−⟨ 𝑁 (𝑧 ′) − 𝑁 (𝑧 ), 𝑧 ′ − 𝑧 ⟩𝐻 ≤ ‖ 𝑁 (𝑧 ′) − 𝑁 (𝑧 )‖2 ‖𝑧 ′ − 𝑧 ‖2 ≤ 2𝐶 B𝑅 ‖𝑧 ′ − 𝑧 ‖22.

Thus Assumption 2.3 (ii) holds on bounded 𝐻 -balls, with 𝐶 𝑅 = 2 𝐶 B𝑅 .We have therefore verified that any reduced model of the form (A.1) satisfying the structural assumptions above belongs to the abstract system class of Section 2. In particular, this includes Fourier–Galerkin truncations of the two-dimensional Navier–Stokes equations on periodic domains, as well as divergence-free Galerkin reductions preserving the incompressible energy identity. Once a feedback operator 𝐼 ℎ satisfying Assumption 2.7 is specified, the full nudging framework applies to these finite-dimensional Galerkin-type models.

Since 𝑢 ∈ 𝐶 1([0 , ∞); R𝑑 ) is deterministic and 𝑁 is locally Lipschitz, the drift 𝑏 (𝑡, ·) is locally Lipschitz uniformly on compact time intervals. The diffusion coefficient 𝜇 Γℎ is constant. # D Proofs for the surrogate extension and post-absorption region 

Proof of Proposition 3.1. Let 

K := {𝑧 ∈ R𝑑 : ‖𝑧 ‖𝑉 ≤ 𝑅 +ext }.

By assumption, ̂︀ 𝐹 𝑀 is locally Lipschitz on an open neighborhood 𝑈 ⊃ K. Since K is compact, ̂︀ 𝐹 𝑀 

is bounded and Lipschitz on K. Moreover, by construction, 

𝜒 (𝑧 ) = 0 whenever ‖𝑧 ‖𝑉 ≥ 𝑅 +ext ,

so supp 𝜒 ⊂ K ⋐ 𝑈 . Define 

𝐺 (𝑧 ) := 𝜒 (𝑧 )̂︀ 𝐹 𝑀 (𝑧 ), 𝑧 ∈ 𝑈. 

Then we can naturally extend 𝐺 to R𝑑 by setting 𝐺 (𝑧 ) = 0 for 𝑧 / ∈ 𝑈 . Since 𝜒 vanishes in a neighborhood of R𝑑 ∖ 𝑈 , this extension remains locally Lipschitz on R𝑑 . Given that 𝐺 vanishes outside supp 𝜒 , and that ̂︀ 𝐹 𝑀 is bounded on K, there exists 𝑀 𝐺 > 0 such that 

‖𝐺 (𝑧 )‖𝐻 ≤ 𝑀 𝐺 , ∀𝑧 ∈ R𝑑 . (D.1) 

54 Next, write 

𝐹 𝑀 (𝑧 ) = 𝐺 (𝑧 ) + (︀ 1 − 𝜒 (𝑧 ))︀(︀ 𝑓 − 𝐵𝑧 )︀ .

The first term is globally defined and locally Lipschitz. The second term is the product of a smooth bounded cutoff and an affine map. Hence 𝐹 𝑀 is locally Lipschitz on R𝑑 . Moreover, using (D.1) ,

0 ≤ 1 − 𝜒 ≤ 1, and the fact that 𝐵 is fixed, we obtain the linear-growth bound 

‖𝐹 𝑀 (𝑧 )‖𝐻 ≤ 𝐶 𝑀 (︀ 1 + ‖𝑧 ‖𝐻 )︀ , ∀𝑧 ∈ R𝑑 , (D.2) for some constant 𝐶 𝑀 > 0.Now fix an arbitrary initial pair (𝑢 0, 𝑣 0) ∈ R𝑑 × R𝑑 . By Proposition 2.12, the true system (3.1) admits a unique global solution 𝑢 ∈ 𝐶 1([0 , ∞); R𝑑 ), with 𝑢 (0) = 𝑢 0. We fix this deterministic trajectory throughout the rest of the proof. The surrogate nudged equation (3.2) can then be written as the nonautonomous ODE 

˙𝑣 = 𝑏 (𝑡, 𝑣 ), 𝑏 (𝑡, 𝑧 ) := 𝐹 𝑀 (𝑧 ) − 𝜇𝐼 ℎ𝑧 + 𝜇𝐼 ℎ𝑢 (𝑡 ).

Since 𝑢 ∈ 𝐶 ([0 , ∞); R𝑑 ) and 𝐼 ℎ is linear, the map 𝑡 ↦ → 𝐼 ℎ𝑢 (𝑡 ) is continuous. Together with the local Lipschitz property of 𝐹 𝑀 , this implies that 𝑏 is continuous in 𝑡 and locally Lipschitz in 𝑧 , uniformly on compact time intervals. Therefore, the standard finite-dimensional ODE theorem gives a unique maximal solution 

𝑣 ∈ 𝐶 1([0 , 𝑇 max ); R𝑑 )

for some 𝑇 max ∈ (0 , ∞].It remains to show that 𝑇 max = ∞. Fix 𝑇 > 0. Since 𝑢 is continuous on [0 , 𝑇 ],

𝑀 𝑢 (𝑇 ) := sup 

> 0≤𝑡 ≤𝑇

‖𝑢 (𝑡 )‖𝐻 < ∞.

Using (D.2) and the 𝐻 -boundedness of 𝐼 ℎ, we have, for 0 ≤ 𝑡 ≤ 𝑇 ,

‖𝑏 (𝑡, 𝑧 )‖𝐻 ≤ 𝐶 𝑀 (︀ 1 + ‖𝑧 ‖𝐻 )︀ + 𝜇𝐶 𝐼 ‖𝑧 ‖𝐻 + 𝜇𝐶 𝐼 𝑀 𝑢 (𝑇 ) ≤ 𝑎 𝑇 + 𝑏 𝑇 ‖𝑧 ‖𝐻 

for suitable constants 𝑎 𝑇 , 𝑏 𝑇 > 0. Hence, along the maximal solution, 

𝑑 𝑑𝑡 ‖𝑣 (𝑡 )‖𝐻 ≤ 𝑎 𝑇 + 𝑏 𝑇 ‖𝑣 (𝑡 )‖𝐻 for a.e. 𝑡 ∈ [0 , min {𝑇, 𝑇 max }).

By Grönwall’s inequality, ‖𝑣 (𝑡 )‖𝐻 remains bounded on every bounded time interval. Therefore no finite-time blow-up can occur, and the maximal solution extends globally. Thus 

𝑣 ∈ 𝐶 1([0 , ∞); R𝑑 ).

Uniqueness follows from the local Lipschitz property of 𝑏 (𝑡, ·) on bounded sets.

# H Proofs for the DSRN-based complexity analysis 

Proof of Proposition 5.16. We first transfer the approximation problem from B* to the unit cube, where the DSRN Sobolev approximation theorem applies, and then pull the resulting approximant back to B*. Throughout the proof, we use the convention that Sobolev spaces on B* are understood through its interior. Since this interior is a bounded Lipschitz domain, there exists a bounded Sobolev extension operator 

𝐸 : 𝑊 𝑚, ∞(B*; 𝐻 ) → 𝑊 𝑚, ∞(𝑄 ; 𝐻 ),

where 𝑄 ⊂ R𝑑 is a cube containing B*, such that 

‖𝐸 g‖𝑊 𝑚, ∞(𝑄 ;𝐻 ) ≤ 𝐶 ext ‖g‖𝑊 𝑚, ∞(B*;𝐻 ) ∀ g ∈ 𝑊 𝑚, ∞(B*; 𝐻 ).

Let 𝑇 : (0 , 1) 𝑑 → 𝑄 be an affine bijection and define 

𝐺 := ( 𝐸𝑆 Δ𝑡 ) ∘ 𝑇 ∈ 𝑊 𝑚, ∞((0 , 1) 𝑑 ; 𝐻 ).

65 By the affine change-of-variables formula for Sobolev norms, there exists a constant 𝐶 𝑇 > 0,depending only on the geometry of 𝑄 , such that 

‖𝐺 ‖𝑊 𝑚, ∞((0 ,1) 𝑑 ;𝐻 ) ≤ 𝐶 𝑇 ‖𝐸𝑆 Δ𝑡 ‖𝑊 𝑚, ∞(𝑄 ;𝐻 ) ≤ 𝐶 𝑇 𝐶 ext ‖𝑆 Δ𝑡 ‖𝑊 𝑚, ∞(B*;𝐻 ).

We now apply [ 57 , Corollary 2] on the unit cube, with 𝑝 = ∞, source regularity 𝑚 , and target Sobolev order 𝑠 , to the 𝐻 ≃ R𝑑 -valued map 𝐺 . Here the 𝐻 -valued Sobolev norm is understood through the equivalent coordinate Sobolev norms. Thus, there exist integers 𝑁 0, 𝐿 0 ∈ N, depending only on 𝑑, 𝑚, 𝑠 , and ‖𝐺 ‖𝑊 𝑚, ∞((0 ,1) 𝑑 ;𝐻 ), such that for every 𝑁 ≥ 𝑁 0 and 𝐿 ≥ 𝐿 0, there exists a DSRN ̃︀ h𝑁,𝐿 on (0 , 1) 𝑑 satisfying 

‖̃︀ h𝑁,𝐿 − 𝐺 ‖𝑊 𝑠, ∞((0 ,1) 𝑑 ;𝐻 ) ≤ 𝐶 ‖𝐺 ‖𝑊 𝑚, ∞((0 ,1) 𝑑 ;𝐻 )𝑁 −2( 𝑚 −𝑠 )/𝑑 𝐿 −2( 𝑚 −𝑠 )/𝑑 ,

and 

width( ̃︀ h𝑁,𝐿 ) ≤ 𝐶 wd 𝑁 log(2 + 𝑁 ), depth( ̃︀ h𝑁,𝐿 ) ≤ 𝐶 dp 𝐿 log(2 + 𝐿 ).

Here 𝐶, 𝐶 wd , 𝐶 dp may depend on the input and output dimensions, the Sobolev orders, and the architectural constants in the DSRN approximation theorem, but not on 𝑁 or 𝐿 .Define

Proof of Proposition 5.18. Fix |𝛼 | ≤ 𝑠 and 1 ≤ ℓ ≤ 𝑑 . This proves (5.21).

# I Choice of the nudging parameter 

The tracking theorems identify qualitative conditions under which nudging is effective, but the constants entering those conditions are not explicitly available in practice. Accordingly, we do not interpret the numerical choice of 𝜇 as approximating a theorem-level optimum. Instead, for each learning route and each observation regime, we choose 𝜇 through a simple calibration step based on one true trajectory generated from an arbitrary initial state, one random initialization of the nudged state, and one representative feedback resolution from the corresponding experiment. More precisely, for each candidate 𝜇 in the prescribed grid, we evolve the surrogate nudged dynamics for this single calibration run and compute the integrated squared tracking error 

𝐽 (𝜇 ) = ∫︁ 

> 𝑇
> 0

‖𝑣 𝜇 (𝑡 ) − 𝑢 (𝑡 )‖22 𝑑𝑡. 

We then choose the value of 𝜇 that minimizes 𝐽 (𝜇 ) in this calibration run and keep it fixed in all subsequent multi-initialization summaries. In this way, the choice of 𝜇 should be viewed as a validation-style selection of a reasonable operating point, rather than as an oracle choice based on the full collection of reported experiments. For the experiments reported here, the resulting values are 𝜇 * = 25 and 𝜇 * = 20 in the direct vector-field learning route, and 𝜇 * = 80 and 𝜇 * = 40 in the solution-map learning route, in the noiseless and noisy-observation settings, respectively.
